2:22:1175:0:14:72:475:700:12:Times:12:Times:C:Baby Busts and Baby Booms: The Fertility Response to Shocks in Dynastic Models
2:23:1253:14:14:72:261:686:12:Times:12:Times:C:Larry E. Jones and Alice Schoonbroodt
2:24:1290:14:14:72:234:672:12:Times:12:Times:C:NBER Working Paper No. 16596
2:25:1318:14:14:72:148:658:12:Times:12:Times:C:December 2010
2:26:1331:14:28:72:197:644:12:Times:12:Times:C:JEL No. E13,J11,J13,O11
2:27:1354:28:28:306:339:616:12:Times:12:Times:C:ABSTRACT
2:28:1362:28:14:72:540:588:12:Times:12:Times:F:Economic demographers have long analyzed fertility cycles. This paper builds a foundation for these
2:29:1461:14:14:72:540:574:12:Times:12:Times:F:cycles in a model of fertility choice with dynastic altruism and aggregate shocks. It is shown that under
2:30:1566:14:14:72:540:560:12:Times:12:Times:F:reasonable parameter values, fertility is pro-cyclical and that, following a shock, fertility continues
2:31:1669:14:14:72:540:546:12:Times:12:Times:F:to cycle endogenously as subsequent cohorts enter retirement. Quantitatively, in the model, the Great
2:32:1770:14:14:72:540:532:12:Times:12:Times:F:Depression generates a large baby bust -- between 38% and 63% of that seen in the U.S. in the 1930s
2:33:1869:14:14:72:540:518:12:Times:12:Times:F:-- which is subsequently followed by a baby boom --  between 53% and 92% of that seen in the U.S.
2:34:1966:14:42:72:134:504:12:Times:12:Times:C:in the 1950s.
2:35:1979:42:14:72:142:462:12:Times:12:Times:C:Larry E. Jones
2:36:1993:14:14:72:198:448:12:Times:12:Times:C:Department of Economics
2:37:2016:14:14:72:189:434:12:Times:12:Times:C:University of Minnesota
2:38:2039:14:14:72:163:420:12:Times:12:Times:C:4-101 Hanson Hall
2:39:2056:14:14:72:193:406:12:Times:12:Times:C:1925 Fourth Street South
2:40:2080:14:14:72:190:392:12:Times:12:Times:C:Minneapolis, MN 55455
2:41:2101:14:14:72:124:378:12:Times:12:Times:C:and NBER
2:42:2109:14:28:72:137:364:12:Times:12:Times:C:lej@umn.edu
2:43:2120:28:14:72:168:336:12:Times:12:Times:C:Alice Schoonbroodt
2:44:2138:14:14:72:203:322:12:Times:12:Times:C:University of Southampton
2:45:2163:14:14:72:196:308:12:Times:12:Times:C:School of Social Sciences
2:46:2188:14:14:72:294:294:12:Times:12:Times:C:Economics Division (58 Murray, Room 3013)
2:47:2229:14:14:72:240:280:12:Times:12:Times:C:Highfield, Southampton SO17 1BJ
2:48:2260:14:14:72:153:266:12:Times:12:Times:C:United Kingdom
2:49:2274:14:0:72:176:252:12:Times:12:Times:C:alicesch@soton.ac.uk
3:50:2294:0:30:71:207:707:17:g_d0_f1:17:g_d0_f1:C:1  Introduction
3:51:2309:30:18:71:539:677:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:Fertility rates in the U.S. varied dramatically over the 20th century. During the first part
3:52:2401:18:18:71:539:659:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:of the century, they continued a drop, begun in the mid 19th century, that demographers
3:53:2488:18:18:71:539:641:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:associate with the Demographic Transition. Total Fertility Rates (TFR) fell from 5.7 children
3:54:2581:18:18:71:539:623:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:per woman to 3.0 over the period from 1850 to 1925. In the yearsthat followed there was an
3:55:2671:18:18:71:540:605:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:even more abrupt drop during the years of the Great Depression, from 3.0 to 2.1. Indeed,
3:56:2759:18:18:71:540:587:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:women in their prime fertility years during the Great Depression had, on average, only 2.2
3:57:2849:18:18:71:539:569:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:children in their entire lifetime.  These women, born between 1905 and 1915, had fewer
3:58:2935:18:18:71:539:551:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:children than any previous cohort (in fact, fertility did not go back down to those levels
3:59:3025:18:17:71:539:533:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:until the 1970s). Following this, fertility rebounded significantly during the 1950s and early
3:60:3119:17:18:71:540:516:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:1960s, the Baby Boom. At its peak, TFR reached 3.6 while Cohort Total Fertility (CTFR)
3:61:3205:18:18:71:322:498:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:showed a similar, but smaller, increase up to 3.2.
3:62:3255:18:18:89:540:480:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:One of the key hypotheses put forward by economic demographers for these large and
3:63:3337:18:18:71:539:462:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:opposite swings is known as the ‘Easterlin hypothesis’, seeEasterlin (1961, 1968, 1978, 1987).
3:64:3431:18:18:71:540:444:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:In a nutshell, the idea behind this hypothesis is that fertility was exceptionally low during the
3:65:3528:18:18:71:539:426:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:Great Depression because of the large negative shock to incomes. Then, in substantial part,
3:66:3619:18:18:71:539:408:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:due to the fact that fertility had been so low during the 1930s, the Baby Boom occurred. The
3:67:3711:18:18:71:540:390:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:mechanism through which low fertility in the past leads to high fertility one generation later
3:68:3805:18:18:71:540:372:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:has usually been attributed to the feedback effects of the resulting unbalanced age-structure
3:69:3898:18:18:71:539:354:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:on relative wages of young fertile workers to older workers in combination with differences
3:70:3989:18:18:71:525:336:12:g_d0_f2:8:g_d0_f3:B:between expected lifetime income relative to ’material aspirations formed in childhood.’1
3:71:4078:18:18:89:540:318:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:In this paper, we build a model of fertility choice combiningfeatures of a stochastic growth
3:72:4170:18:18:71:539:300:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:model with a model of fertility choice with dynastic altruism `a la Barro-Becker (Becker
3:73:4258:18:17:71:539:282:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:and Barro, 1988, and Barro and Becker, 1989).2In addition to the stochastic component,
3:74:4344:17:18:71:539:265:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:the model extends existing Barro-Becker type models to include multiple periods in the
3:75:4430:18:18:71:540:247:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:working life. For simplicity, we assume that labor is the only factor of production (except in
3:76:4524:18:18:71:540:229:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:Section 5.3) and that different age-groups are perfect substitutes in production (up to age-
3:77:4616:18:19:71:539:211:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:specific productivity levels). Thus, we abstract from feedback effects of relative cohort sizes
3:78:4711:19:15:85:540:192:7:g_d0_f4:10:g_d0_f5:F:1The so called ‘Easterlin hypothesis’ has developed over Easterlin’s own work and has been interpreted
3:79:4813:15:12:71:540:177:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:in more than one way by other authors along the way. For an excellent overview until 1998, see Macunovich
3:80:4918:12:12:71:539:165:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:(1998). She ‘focuses just on the fertility aspects of the Easterlin hypothesis’, which is our focus as well. For
3:81:5030:12:9:71:347:153:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:a more general overview, see Macunovich and Easterlin (2008).
3:82:5091:9:15:85:540:144:7:g_d0_f4:10:g_d0_f5:F:2Some have put forth the idea that the baby boom was a consequence of low fertility during the Great
3:83:5191:15:12:71:539:129:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:Depression – i.e., the baby boom was ‘catching up.’ However,completed fertility (CTFR) was low for both
3:84:5294:12:12:71:540:117:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:the women immediately preceding and immediately followingthe baby boom and hence, this cannot be true
3:85:5395:12:12:71:539:105:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:at the level of the individual mother. It can, in principle, hold across cohorts in a dynastic model. This
3:86:5501:12:0:71:499:93:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:distinction is relevant in our analysis and is one of our motivations for studying a dynastic model.
4:87:5602:0:18:71:540:707:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:on relative wages. In this model, we study, both qualitatively and quantitatively, fluctuations
4:88:5697:18:18:71:540:689:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:in fertility, triggered by (large) income shocks using preference parameter configurations in
4:89:5790:18:18:71:381:671:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:line with the standard growth and business-cycle literatures.
4:90:5851:18:18:89:539:653:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:We show that both of the effects originally discussed by Easterlin, the large reactions
4:91:5938:18:18:71:540:635:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:of fertility to large income shocks, and the oscillations thereafter, are present in the model
4:92:6032:18:18:71:539:617:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:(despite the absence of feedback effects through wages). In particular, we find that fertility
4:93:6126:18:18:71:539:599:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:is procyclical as long as most of the costs of children are in terms of goods, or as long as
4:94:6218:18:18:71:539:581:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:there is sufficient curvature in the period utility function.On top of this, the policy function
4:95:6314:18:18:71:539:563:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:for current fertility as a function of past fertility that comes out of our model is negatively
4:96:6409:18:18:71:540:545:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:sloped around the steady state. We find that that the dynamicsof adjustment following a
4:97:6496:18:18:71:539:527:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:movement off a balanced growth path is one of dampened oscillations. Putting these two
4:98:6582:18:18:71:539:509:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:effects together, in the model, a large negative income shockis met with a contemporaneous
4:99:6672:18:17:71:539:491:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:reduction in fertility followed by a baby boom a generation later. Thus, in the model the
4:100:6761:17:18:71:529:474:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:Great Depression would trigger a baby bust, followed by a baby boom a generation later.
4:101:6848:18:18:89:539:456:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:In our model, children are partly a consumption good (Barro-Becker preferences) and
4:102:6931:18:18:71:539:438:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:partly an investment good (future labor force) for the dynasty. Comparing our results with
4:103:7021:18:18:71:539:420:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:those standard in growth models gives substantial intuition. The role of the capital stock(s),
4:104:7115:18:18:71:539:402:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:is played by the age distribution of the population, while the number of births plays the role
4:105:7209:18:18:71:539:384:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:of investment. Then, as is standard in growth models (for standard consumption smoothing
4:106:7297:18:18:71:540:366:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:reasons), periods when productivity is particularly high are times when investment is high.
4:107:7388:18:18:71:540:348:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:Here this translates to: fertility is high during periods ofhigh productivity – an income
4:108:7477:18:18:71:540:330:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:boom generates a baby boom. Correspondingly, an income bustgenerates a baby bust. In
4:109:7561:18:18:71:539:312:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:addition, the larger is the desire to smooth consumption, the larger the elasticity of fertility
4:110:7657:18:18:71:539:294:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:to income shocks. There is one subtlety that needs to be addedwith this interpretation.
4:111:7744:18:18:71:539:276:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:This is that, when the cost of a new child is primarily in termsof time, good times are both
4:112:7836:18:17:71:539:258:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:good times to save for the future (invest) and times when thatinvestment is most expensive.
4:113:7927:17:18:71:540:241:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:As a result, in this case, fertility is only procyclical whenthere is sufficient desire to smooth
4:114:8024:18:18:71:338:223:12:g_d0_f2:8:g_d0_f3:B:consumption (i.e., more curvature than log utility).3
4:115:8077:18:18:89:539:205:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:A similar intuition gives us insight as to why the model exhibits dampened oscillations.
4:116:8165:18:18:71:540:187:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:This has to do with the specification of the implicit depreciation rates of the age-specific
4:117:8257:18:18:71:539:169:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:populations. When the stock is the number of people, the counterpart of depreciation is
4:118:8344:18:19:71:539:151:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:movement out of the work force. Thus, fertility (i.e., investment) will be higher in periods
4:119:8436:19:15:85:540:132:7:g_d0_f4:10:g_d0_f5:F:3Notice that one important feature here is that income istemporarilyhigh or low,relativeto trend. As
4:120:8536:15:12:72:539:117:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:shown by Jones and Schoonbroodt (2010), apermanentincrease in productivity growth has a permanent
4:121:8633:12:12:72:540:105:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f5:F:negativeeffect on fertility under the parameter values that generatepro-cyclical fertility here. Thus, the
4:122:8739:12:12:72:540:93:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:important distinction between temporary and permanent changes in productivity shocks is just as important
4:123:8844:12:0:72:328:81:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:here as it is in standard growth and business cycle models.
5:124:8904:0:18:71:539:707:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:when a relatively large share of workers are nearing retirement age. Because of this effect, a
5:125:8998:18:18:71:540:689:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:baby bust now means that the next generation’s workforce is primarily older workers – more
5:126:9088:18:18:71:540:671:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:workers will be retiring. Consequently, investment will behigher in response – a baby boom
5:127:9178:18:18:71:124:653:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:will occur.
5:128:9189:18:18:89:539:635:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:On the quantitative side, we use a calibrated version of the model to simulate the size of
5:129:9279:18:18:71:540:617:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:the response of fertility to productivity shocks, both contemporaneous and delayed. After
5:130:9368:18:18:71:539:599:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:calibrating to U.S. averages, we find that the contemporaneous response to a 1 percent
5:131:9454:18:18:71:540:581:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:deviation in productivity lies in the range of 1 to 1.7 percent depending on the nature of the
5:132:9548:18:18:71:539:563:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:costs of children (generally, responses are smaller when the costs of children are primarily in
5:133:9643:18:18:71:540:545:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:terms of parental time) while the elasticity one period later lies between 0.94 and 1.5. This
5:134:9736:18:18:71:539:527:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:implies that the response of (completed) fertility to a ‘standard recession’ (say, productivity
5:135:9831:18:18:71:540:509:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:is 5 percent below trend for 2 years) is relatively small – of the order of 0.02 to 0.04 children
5:136:9928:18:17:71:540:491:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:per woman with a subsequent baby boom of similar size. However, we find that the reduction
5:137:10018:17:18:71:539:474:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:in fertility implied by the model as a response to the 12 percent decrease in productivity
5:138:10108:18:18:71:539:456:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:during the Great Depression is between 38 and 63 percent of the observed pre-WWII baby
5:139:10194:18:18:71:539:438:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:bust in Total Fertility Rates (TFR). Moreover, the subsequent endogenous fluctuations in
5:140:10282:18:18:71:539:420:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:fertility triggered by this bust, in conjunction with the productivity boom in the 1950s and
5:141:10374:18:18:71:497:402:12:g_d0_f2:8:g_d0_f3:B:1960s, captures between 53 and 92 percent of the post-WWII baby boom in TFR.4
5:142:10451:18:18:89:539:384:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:Since both the Depression and the Baby Boom are phenomena that occurred in many
5:143:10530:18:18:71:540:366:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:different countries, we go on to study one obvious implication of our model. If the mechanisms
5:144:10624:18:18:71:540:348:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:that we study are important, it should be true that countriesthat had deeper depressions in
5:145:10715:18:18:71:539:330:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:the 1930s should also have larger baby busts in the 1930s, andthat those with larger baby
5:146:10804:18:18:71:539:312:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:busts should experience larger baby booms in the 1950s. In Section 6, we show that this
5:147:10891:18:18:71:539:294:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:is indeed true, conditional on the economic circumstances in the 1950s. These observations
5:148:10981:18:18:71:210:276:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:further support our theory.
5:149:11008:18:17:89:540:258:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:Many other demographers and economists have studied fluctuations in fertility but they
5:150:11094:17:18:71:540:241:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:have focused on different channels. First, operationally, the mechanisms that are the driving
5:151:11187:18:18:71:539:223:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:forces behind our results are considerably different than those emphasized by Easterlin in his
5:152:11281:18:18:71:540:205:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:work. For example, he emphasizes differences between expected lifetime income relative to
5:153:11370:18:18:71:540:187:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:’material aspirations formed in childhood.’ Here, the basic mechanisms are best understood
5:154:11460:18:18:71:533:169:12:g_d0_f2:8:g_d0_f3:B:as variants of standard effects of growth models – the desire to smooth consumption, etc.5
5:155:11550:18:18:89:539:151:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:Second, following Easterlin many authors have analyzed thedynamics of age-structured
5:156:11634:18:15:85:540:133:7:g_d0_f4:10:g_d0_f5:F:4Realistically, it seems unlikely that fertility decisionsare affected by quarter to quarter fluctuations in
5:157:11742:15:12:71:539:118:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:productivity (as addressed in the business cycle literature). This is consistent with our quantitative findings.
5:158:11854:12:9:71:418:106:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:Rather, a prolonged boom or bust is required for the fertility effect to be large.
5:159:11936:9:15:85:540:97:7:g_d0_f4:10:g_d0_f5:F:5Feichtinger and Dockner (1990) assume a positive relationship between births and the difference between
5:160:12040:15:0:71:469:82:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:actual and expected consumption, which is more in line with Easterlin’s original argument.
6:161:12131:0:18:71:540:707:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:populations that results when it isassumedthat the relationship between fertility today and
6:162:12222:18:18:72:539:689:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:past fertility has a negative slope, see Lee (1974) and Samuelson (1976) for early analysis
6:163:12313:18:18:72:539:671:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:of dynamic population systems of this kind.6In contrast, we provide a choice-theoretic
6:164:12399:18:18:72:211:653:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:foundation for these cycles.
6:165:12427:18:18:89:539:635:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:Third, we assume that different ages of workers are perfect substitutes in production.
6:166:12513:18:18:72:540:617:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:Nevertheless, fertility cycles are generated through the curvature of preferences. This shows
6:167:12606:18:18:72:539:599:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:that feedback effects from the age-structure on wages are notnecessary to generate popula-
6:168:12696:18:18:72:539:581:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:tion cycles. This is an alternative to Easterlin’s feedbackeffect which, as suggested by Lee
6:169:12788:18:18:72:539:563:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:(2008), can be generated by using a CES production specification, where different age-groups
6:170:12880:18:18:72:210:545:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:are not perfect substitutes.
6:171:12908:18:18:89:539:527:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:Further, the mechanisms that we highlight here are also complementary to those explored
6:172:12995:18:18:72:540:509:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:in other papers that have used choice-theoretic dynamic macroeconomic models to study
6:173:13080:18:17:72:540:491:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:the Baby Boom. Three key examples are Greenwood et al. (2005), Doepke et al. (2007) and
6:174:13167:17:18:72:539:474:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:Albanesi and Olivetti (2010). In Greenwood et al. (2005), the channel that is highlighted is
6:175:13259:18:18:72:540:456:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:the effects of the drastic improvement in technologies of home production that took place in
6:176:13351:18:18:72:539:438:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:the post WWII period. In essence, these improvements temporarily made having children
6:177:13436:18:18:72:539:420:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:cheaper generating the boom. In Doepke et al. (2007), they emphasize the relatively high
6:178:13524:18:18:72:540:402:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:female participation rate that took place during WWII. This, because of learning on the job,
6:179:13616:18:18:72:539:384:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:made that specific cohort of women relatively high productivity. Because of this, the cohort
6:180:13708:18:18:72:539:366:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:of women entering their 20s in the 1950s faced a relatively tough job market causing them
6:181:13797:18:18:72:540:348:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:to delay entering the workforce and have larger than normal families. Albanesi and Olivetti
6:182:13888:18:18:72:539:330:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:(2010) emphasize the decrease in maternal mortality as a cause of the baby boom. This
6:183:13973:18:18:72:540:312:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:decrease in mortality also increased the incentive to invest in human capital, triggering the
6:184:14066:18:18:72:539:294:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:return to low fertility in the 1970s. Thus, the primary channels emphasized in those papers
6:185:14157:18:18:72:539:276:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:differ from ours. One advantage of our approach is that it is also able to capture the sizeable
6:186:14252:18:17:72:398:258:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:downward movement, relative to trend, in fertility in the 1930s.
6:187:14316:17:18:89:539:241:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:Finally, our findings can rationalize the observations in Butz and Ward (1979) as follows.
6:188:14406:18:18:72:539:223:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:In brief, they find that fertility, while procyclical, has become less responsive to business
6:189:14499:18:18:72:540:205:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:cycle frequency fluctuations over time. In our model, this can happen for two reasons. The
6:190:14589:18:18:72:540:187:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:first comes from the fact that the size of the effects that we findare smaller when the
6:191:14676:18:18:72:539:169:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:costs of children are in terms of time. Since there has been a large increase in labor force
6:192:14768:18:18:72:539:151:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:participation among married women, this causes the time cost of children to be increasingly
6:193:14859:18:18:72:540:133:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:important. Second, due to continuing fluctuations triggered by the Great Depression, the
6:194:14947:18:18:72:540:115:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:fertility response to productivity shocks is mitigated. Inour data analysis, we show that,
6:195:15037:18:0:85:452:97:7:g_d0_f4:10:g_d0_f5:A:6Feichtinger and Sorger (1989) extend Samuelson’s initial model to continuous time.
7:196:15121:0:18:71:540:707:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:controlling for productivity shocks in the past is crucial to find a positive correlation of
7:197:15213:18:18:71:540:689:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:fertility with contemporary shocks. It is yet to be seen how large the fertility response to
7:198:15305:18:18:71:539:671:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:the most recent recession will be, though it does go in the direction predicted by the theory
7:199:15398:18:18:71:274:653:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:(see, for example, Sobotka et al., 2010).
7:200:15439:18:18:89:539:635:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:In Section 2 we review the data for the U.S. since 1900. The model is laid out in Section 3
7:201:15530:18:18:71:539:617:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:and the analytic results are presented. In Section 4, we explore the quantitative implications
7:202:15624:18:18:71:539:599:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:of a calibrated version of the model compared to U.S. data.  InSection 5 we study the
7:203:15709:18:18:71:540:581:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:sensitivity of our results to some basic changes in our assumptions.  Finally, we present
7:204:15798:18:35:71:385:563:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:international evidence in Section 6 while Section 7 concludes.
7:205:15860:35:25:71:180:528:17:g_d0_f1:17:g_d0_f1:C:2  U.S. data
7:206:15872:25:18:71:540:503:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:In this section, we lay out the basic facts about the time paths of productivity and fertility in
7:207:15969:18:18:71:539:485:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:the U.S. over the 20th century. We begin with the facts pertaining to the growth in produc-
7:208:16060:18:18:71:540:467:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:tivity using a consistent measure for total factor productivity (TFP) and labor productivity
7:209:16152:18:18:71:539:449:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:(LP) from Chari et al. (2007). As most economists know, this period is one of more or less
7:210:16242:18:17:71:539:431:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:continued growth in productivity with a few interruptions.The most significant of these is
7:211:16332:17:18:71:540:414:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:the Great Depression. Figure 1 shows the natural logarithmsof TFP and LP over the period
7:212:16420:18:19:71:167:396:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:from 1901 to 2000.
7:213:16438:19:23:89:467:377:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:The facts about productivity over this period can be described as follows:
7:214:16512:23:24:86:244:354:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:1. the continual upward trend;
7:215:16542:24:25:86:497:330:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:2. the marked decline below trend that took place in the 1930sand early 1940s;
7:216:16620:25:24:86:297:305:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:3. the return to trend in the early 1950s;
7:217:16662:24:24:86:487:281:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:4. the significant increase above trend that took place in the1950s and 1960s;
7:218:16740:24:31:86:321:257:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:5. the productivity slowdown since the 1970s.
7:219:16785:31:18:89:539:226:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:This timing of the movements of productivity around trend fits well with the movements
7:220:16871:18:18:71:540:208:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:in fertility seen in the data. Figure 2 shows the time path of the Total Fertility Rate (TFR)
7:221:16964:18:18:71:539:190:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:and Cohort Total Fertility Rates (CTFR) (by birthyear of mother +23 years) over the period
7:222:17054:18:18:71:539:172:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:from 1850 to 2000. We have two time series for TFR, which calculates how many children a
7:223:17142:18:18:71:540:154:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:woman would have over her lifetime if current age-specific fertility rates were to prevail in the
7:224:17239:18:18:71:539:136:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:future. The first series is the one prepared by Haines (1994) using Census data and hence is
7:225:17331:18:18:71:540:118:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:available only every 10 years. The second comes from the Natality Statistics Analysis from
7:226:17421:18:18:71:540:100:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:National Center for Health Statistics. It is available at annual frequencies, but only since
7:227:17513:18:0:71:539:82:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:1917. The CTFR series comes from Jones and Tertilt (2008) andcounts how many children
8:228:17599:0:190:91:520:700:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:Figure 1: Total Factor Productivity and Labor Productivity, 1901-2000 (1929=100)

8:241:17836:18:18:71:539:434:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:is equivalent to adding age-specific fertility rates pertaining to a particular cohort of women

8:243:17983:18:18:89:540:398:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:At the beginning of the period, fertility is still in the midst of what is known to demog-
8:244:18073:18:18:71:540:380:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:raphers as the demographic transition, the marked fall in fertility (and mortality) that has
8:245:18165:18:18:71:540:362:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:occurred in all developed countries.  This fall acceleratesfrom the late 1920s to the mid
8:246:18254:18:18:71:540:344:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:1930s. Fertility then increases to reach its peak in the babyboom of the 1950s and 1960s.
8:247:18343:18:24:71:300:326:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:It appears that a good description would be:
8:248:18387:24:14:86:539:302:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:1. high, and fairly constantly decreasing fertility from 1850 until 1925, when it reaches a

8:250:18514:24:15:86:540:264:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:2. an acceleration of the rate at which fertility is falling between 1925 and 1933 (from
8:251:18602:15:24:101:225:249:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:?:TFR=3.0 to TFR=2.1);
8:252:18622:24:15:86:540:225:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:3. constant, but low, fertility over the period from 1933 to 1940, with the level at about
8:253:18712:15:24:101:153:210:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:?:TFR=2.2;
8:254:18720:24:25:86:497:186:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:4. rapidly rising fertility from 1940 to 1957, with TFR goingfrom 2.2 up to 3.7;
8:255:18800:25:24:86:403:161:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:5. high, stable fertility from 1957 to 1961 at about TFR=3.6;
8:256:18861:24:24:86:484:137:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:6. a rapid decrease from 1961 to 1976, with TFR going from 3.6 down to 1.7;
8:257:18936:24:15:86:539:113:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:7. a slight increase and then stable low fertility over the remainder of the period, with

11:331:22229:0:25:71:191:707:17:g_d0_f1:17:g_d0_f1:C:3  The model
11:332:22241:25:18:71:540:682:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:In this section, we lay out a model of the response of fertility to period by period stochastic
11:333:22336:18:18:71:540:664:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:movements in productivity. To do this, we use a model of fertility based on that developed
11:334:22426:18:18:71:539:646:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:in Becker and Barro (1988) and Barro and Becker (1989) (Barro-Becker henceforth). The
11:335:22511:18:18:71:540:628:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:simplification that we make is to assume that there is no physical (or human) capital in the
11:336:22603:18:18:71:539:610:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:model. Thus, the flow of income is solely due to wage income.7On the other hand, we add
11:337:22690:18:29:71:539:592:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:a stochastic component as well as an explicit age-structureto the basic Barro-Becker model.
11:338:22781:29:20:71:196:563:14:g_d0_f1:14:g_d0_f1:C:3.1  Model setup
11:339:22797:20:18:71:540:543:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:A period is 20 years. Every person lives for four periods, oneas a child andT= 3 periods as
11:340:22888:18:18:72:539:525:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:an adult. There is an initial age distribution of the population given by (N30,N20,N10) where
11:341:22981:18:18:72:539:507:12:g_d0_f8:12:g_d0_f2:B:N30is the number of initial old (i.e., their age in periodt= 0 isa= 3). We will normalize
11:342:23070:18:18:71:205:489:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:by assuming thatN30= 1.
11:343:23093:18:18:89:540:471:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:Children (agea= 0) do nothing. At agea= 1, young workers consume, have children
11:344:23172:18:18:71:540:453:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:and supply one unit of labor (net of time spent to raise children) to earn a wage,w1t. At age
11:345:23265:18:18:71:539:435:12:g_d0_f8:12:g_d0_f2:B:a= 2, old workers consume and supply one unit of labor inelastically to earn a wage,w2t,
11:346:23353:18:18:71:539:417:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:but are no longer fertile. In the last period of their lives, agea= 3, agents are retired and
11:347:23446:18:18:71:143:399:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:only consume.
11:348:23459:18:18:89:539:381:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:Adults care about consumption, the number of children and their children’s future utility.
11:349:23549:18:18:71:539:363:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:Following the original Barro-Becker formulation, we assume that the utility of a person who
11:350:23640:18:32:71:507:345:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:was born in periodt−1 and whose first period as an adult is in periodtis given by:
11:351:23722:32:33:72:365:313:12:g_d0_f2:8:g_d0_f3:B:(1)U1t=V1t+φβg(nt)U1t+1

11:353:23832:18:18:72:540:262:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:that point forward,V1tis the utility this person gets from his own path of consumption,ntis
11:354:23923:18:18:72:540:244:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:the number of children that he has andU1t+1is the utility that his typical child will receive.

13:455:28442:22:0:71:306:82:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:(2010) or Schoonbroodt and Tertilt (2010) for details.
14:456:28498:0:18:89:540:707:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:As is usually true in models with exogenous, trend growth, solutions can be obtained by
14:457:28585:18:18:71:539:689:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:solving a related model with no growth and a different discount factor. Thus, the solution
14:458:28675:18:18:71:540:671:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:toP(γ,β;{Na0},s0) can be obtained directly from the solution toP(1,ˆβ;{Na0},s0) – i.e.,
14:459:28762:18:18:72:539:653:12:g_d0_f8:12:g_d0_f2:B:γ= 1 (no growth) and the discount factor,ˆβ, depends onγ,β,σ,andη. Because of this
14:460:28844:18:18:72:539:635:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:result, we abstract from trend growth through most of the remainder of the paper. In those
14:461:28934:18:18:72:539:617:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:cases where the solution to the model depends on the discountfactor, we will use this result
14:462:29026:18:18:72:434:599:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:to calibrate to the appropriate discount factor in the detrended model.
14:463:29097:18:0:89:405:581:12:g_d0_f2:8:g_d0_f9:A:Let us introduce one additional piece of notation. Letnbt≡N
14:464:29156:0:13:406:408:590:6:g_d0_f16:6:g_d0_f16:?:bt
14:465:29158:13:14:392:540:577:8:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f2:A:0.5N1tbe the number of births
14:466:29187:14:18:71:539:563:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:per woman. This is the model quantity that we will identify with the Cohort Total Fertility
14:467:29278:18:29:71:539:545:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:Rate (CTFR) in the data, while TFR will be a weighted average over several cohort’s fertility.

14:514:30456:18:18:71:539:159:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:people in older generations. Thus, ifgis increasing (decreasing) inN,g(Nat) is decreasing

14:518:30817:18:0:72:460:88:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:groups increasing per capita consumption. This leads to a lower value ofu′.

15:549:32609:18:18:72:464:263:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:in theAkmodel – are expensive. Other than that, the analogy is very close.

16:608:34484:0:22:272:282:342:12:g_d0_f11:12:g_d0_f11:?:
16:609:34485:22:0:272:282:320:12:g_d0_f11:12:g_d0_f11:?:
16:610:34486:0:0:284:290:330:12:g_d0_f8:12:g_d0_f8:C:s
16:611:34487:0:13:291:297:343:12:g_d0_f11:12:g_d0_f11:?:[
16:612:34488:13:0:297:332:330:12:g_d0_f8:8:g_d0_f9:?:w1+w
16:613:34492:0:12:332:336:338:6:g_d0_f14:6:g_d0_f14:?:2
16:614:34493:12:0:329:334:326:8:g_d0_f9:8:g_d0_f9:?:n
16:615:34494:0:13:338:344:343:12:g_d0_f11:12:g_d0_f11:?:]
16:616:34495:13:21:346:387:330:12:g_d0_f10:8:g_d0_f13:?:−θ(s)n′
16:617:34502:21:0:332:339:309:12:g_d0_f8:12:g_d0_f8:C:T
16:618:34503:0:22:389:399:342:12:g_d0_f11:12:g_d0_f11:?:
16:619:34504:22:0:389:399:320:12:g_d0_f11:12:g_d0_f11:?:
16:620:34505:0:23:400:405:341:8:g_d0_f9:8:g_d0_f9:?:σ
16:621:34506:23:41:407:480:318:12:g_d0_f8:12:g_d0_f8:C:β≡RHS(n′).
16:622:34516:41:18:71:539:277:12:g_d0_f8:12:g_d0_f2:B:LHS(n′) is increasing inn′, withLHS(0) = 0 ifEˆV1(0,s) =∞. Also,RHS(n′) is decreasing
16:623:34601:18:18:72:448:259:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:inn′with a positive intercept atn′= 0. Thus, there is a unique solution.
16:624:34673:18:18:89:539:241:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:To see the behavior ofn′as a function of the shock, we consider the two extreme cases,
16:625:34759:18:18:72:539:223:12:g_d0_f8:12:g_d0_f8:F:θ(s) =θ, a goods cost, and a time cost,θ(s) =bsw1. In the first case,θ(s) =θ,RHS
16:626:34839:18:18:72:539:205:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:shifts up whensgoes up whileLHSis unchanged. Thus,n′is increasing ins– fertility is
16:627:34922:18:18:72:539:187:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:procyclical. This is a pure income effect which is larger, thelargerσ, i.e. the desire to smooth
16:628:35018:18:18:72:539:169:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f8:B:consumption. Also, it follows thatn′is linear ins. On the other hand, whenθ(s) =bsw1,
16:629:35103:18:17:72:539:151:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:bothLHSandRHSshift up whensincreases. The effect onRHSis still the pure income
16:630:35181:17:18:72:540:134:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:effect, which tends to increase fertility and is increasing inσ,sσ. The effect onLHSis
16:631:35267:18:18:72:539:116:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:the substitution effect because whensis high, fertility is temporarily more expensive which
16:632:35358:18:18:72:539:98:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:tends to decrease fertility. This effect is linear ins. Ifσ >1, the income effect dominates, i.e.
16:633:35455:18:0:72:540:80:12:g_d0_f8:12:g_d0_f2:B:RHSshifts up by more thanLHS. Thus, againn′is increasing ins– fertility is procyclical.
17:634:35544:0:18:71:433:707:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:If,σ <1 the substitution effect dominates – fertility is countercyclical.
17:635:35617:18:18:89:539:689:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:To see how current fertility (per young adult),n′, depends on past fertility,n, notice that
17:636:35708:18:18:71:539:671:12:g_d0_f8:12:g_d0_f2:B:RHSshifts down when the current state,n, increases whileLHSis independent ofn. Thus
17:637:35791:18:18:71:540:653:12:g_d0_f8:12:g_d0_f2:B:n′decreases whennincreases, generating cycles. This crucially depends onw2>0. If there
17:638:35877:18:18:72:539:635:12:g_d0_f2:8:g_d0_f3:B:is only one period of working life (as in standard Barro-Becker models), this effect is zero.10
17:639:35972:18:28:89:379:617:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:We summarize these results in the following proposition:
17:640:36028:28:24:72:301:589:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f7:C:Proposition 1.Current fertility,n′(n,s), is
17:641:36071:24:18:85:395:565:12:g_d0_f7:12:g_d0_f7:A:1. a. procyclical ifθ(w1) =θ, or ifθ(w1) =bsw1andσ >1;
17:642:36125:18:21:101:331:547:12:g_d0_f7:12:g_d0_f2:?:b. countercyclical ifθ(w1) =bsw1andσ <1;
17:643:36165:21:17:85:368:526:12:g_d0_f7:12:g_d0_f2:A:2. a. independent of last period’s fertility,n, ifw2= 0;
17:644:36221:17:24:101:361:509:12:g_d0_f7:12:g_d0_f7:C:b. decreasing in last period’s fertility,n, ifw2>0.
17:645:36272:24:28:89:539:485:12:g_d0_f7:12:g_d0_f7:A:Thus, ifw2>0the model generates endogenous cycles, triggered by productivity shocks.
17:646:36356:28:18:89:540:457:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:The intuition for why fertility is procyclical in this modelis similar to that in many growth
17:647:36449:18:18:72:539:439:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:models.  Here, fertility plays the role of an investment goodand the usual consumption
17:648:36535:18:18:72:539:421:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:smoothing logic implies that when the shock is high, investment should be high so as to
17:649:36622:18:18:72:539:403:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:offset the effects of future shocks on consumption. This argument is direct when the cost of
17:650:36714:18:18:72:540:385:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:children is a goods cost. It is tempered when the cost is a timecost by a second effect. This
17:651:36807:18:18:72:539:367:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:is that the cost of the investment good is also higher than average when the shock is high.
17:652:36898:18:18:72:540:349:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:Thus, whether or not it is a good idea to invest in those periods depends on how strong the
17:653:36989:18:18:72:539:331:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:desire is to smooth consumption. When this force is strong –σis large – the consumption
17:654:37076:18:18:72:539:313:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:smoothing effect is large relative to the cost effect and fertility is procyclical. Thus, the more
17:655:37174:18:18:72:539:295:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:important the time cost in raising children, the more procyclical is the cost of children itself.
17:656:37271:18:17:72:539:277:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:That is, whenever productivity is high, the cost of childrenis also high and vice versa. This
17:657:37364:17:18:72:540:260:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:dampens the procyclicality of fertility and, indeed, when the desire to smooth consumption
17:658:37454:18:18:72:310:242:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:is very low, fertility actually is countercyclical.
17:659:37505:18:18:89:540:224:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:Some intuition for the fertility cycles in point 2 of the proposition can also be obtained
17:660:37595:18:18:72:539:206:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:by analogy with growth models. Here, what we find is a source ofendogenous cycles. The
17:661:37681:18:18:72:539:188:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:relevant analogy from capital theory here is to consider a model in which depreciation is not
17:662:37774:18:18:72:540:170:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:constant. Here, we have an extreme version. Capital (i.e., people) that is built in periodt−1
17:663:37867:18:18:72:539:152:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:has full productive capacity in periodtand periodt+ 1 – there is no depreciation between
17:664:37955:18:18:72:539:134:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:periodstandt+ 1 – but has zero productive capacity in periodt+ 2. Thus, age-specific
17:665:38039:18:18:71:539:116:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:depreciation rates here would beδ1= 0 andδ2= 1 – no depreciation after one period,
17:666:38121:18:15:81:540:98:7:g_d0_f4:10:g_d0_f5:F:10Note that since the number of birthsper woman(CTFR) isnb=n/0.5, all these comparative statics go
17:667:38219:15:0:72:179:83:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:through without change.
18:668:38244:0:18:71:539:707:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:full depreciation after two. In a situation like this, whenn=N1/N2is higher than usual,
18:669:38331:18:18:71:540:689:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:the planner expects next periods depreciation rate to be lower than usual (becauseN2is
18:670:38417:18:18:71:539:671:12:g_d0_f2:8:g_d0_f3:B:relatively low). Because of this, to smooth consumption, current investment –n′=Nb/N1
18:671:38502:18:18:71:539:653:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:– will be relatively low. Thus,n′is low whennis high. When there is only one period of
18:672:38588:18:18:71:539:635:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:working life, the depreciation rate is always 100 percent and hence this effect is not present.
18:673:38683:18:18:71:540:617:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:Because of this, fertility is independent of last period’s fertility.  The example with one
18:674:38774:18:18:71:540:599:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:period working life,w2t= 0, is interesting because it corresponds most closely to that of the
18:675:38867:18:18:72:540:581:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:original Barro-Becker model while the model with more than one period productive life is
18:676:38955:18:18:72:152:563:12:g_d0_f2:8:g_d0_f3:B:more realistic.11
18:677:38972:18:18:89:540:545:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:Thus, according to our theory, while the baby bust in the 1930s may be explained by
18:678:39055:18:18:72:539:527:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:the Great Depression, the baby boom in the 1950s is first and foremost a response to low
18:679:39143:18:35:72:172:509:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:fertility in the past.
18:680:39165:35:25:72:270:474:17:g_d0_f1:17:g_d0_f1:C:4  Quantitative results
18:681:39188:25:18:72:539:449:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:In this section, we use the facts about the time paths of productivity and fertility in the
18:682:39279:18:17:72:540:431:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:U.S. over the 20th century laid out in Section 2 to perform quantitative experiments on
18:683:39366:17:18:72:539:414:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:the model. To do this, we calibrate parameters to selected moments of our data. We then
18:684:39453:18:18:72:540:396:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:use this model to explore two kinds of questions.  First, whatdoes the calibrated model
18:685:39540:18:18:72:540:378:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:say about the size of responses to shocks to productivity – e.g., what is the elasticity of
18:686:39631:18:18:72:539:360:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:fertility with respect to a productivity shock? In keeping with the theoretical results of the
18:687:39725:18:18:72:539:342:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:previous section we study both the current response to a shock and also the lagged response
18:688:39816:18:18:72:540:324:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:one generation later due to the misalignment of the age structure of the workforce. Second,
18:689:39907:18:18:72:540:306:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:based on the estimated policy functions from the calibratedmodel we study the predicted
18:690:39994:18:18:72:538:288:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:response to a productivity shock like those seen in US history – e.g., the Great Depression.
18:691:40086:18:18:89:540:270:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:We find that the answers to these quantitative questions alsodepends on the nature of
18:692:40171:18:18:72:539:252:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:the costs of children. Because of this, we give results for two alternative specifications. These
18:693:40268:18:18:72:539:234:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:are: (1) all costs are time costs (θ(s) =bw1s); (2) all costs of raising children are goods costs
18:694:40365:18:18:71:539:216:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:(θ(s) =θ). As noted above, these two specifications are qualitatively different in that with
18:695:40457:18:17:71:540:198:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:a time cost, the costs of children are higher in productivitybooms than in busts. Reality
18:696:40546:17:18:71:388:181:12:g_d0_f2:8:g_d0_f3:B:probably lies somewhere inbetween these two extreme cases.12
18:697:40606:18:18:89:540:163:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:We find that the quantitative responses in the model are economically significant in all
18:698:40694:18:16:81:540:145:7:g_d0_f4:10:g_d0_f5:F:11In Section 5.4 we add a third period of working life to show that results, though qualitatively more
18:699:40796:16:8:71:263:129:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:complicated, are very similar quantitatively.
18:700:40841:8:16:81:539:121:7:g_d0_f4:10:g_d0_f5:F:12Indeed, the time cost may have become more relevant over timeas women entered the labor force. Since
18:701:40943:16:12:71:539:105:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:women tend to be the parent taking care of children, the relevant opportunity cost is only procyclical if the
18:702:41052:12:0:71:384:93:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:latter are actually working whenever they are not busy raising children.
19:703:41126:0:18:71:540:707:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:cases. For example, the elasticity of fertility to a contemporaneous productivity shocks lies
19:704:41219:18:18:71:539:689:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:between 1 and 1.7,13while the elasticity one period later lies between 0.94 and 1.5. Standard
19:705:41312:18:18:71:539:671:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:recessions will have rather modest effects on fertility, however. Large recessions, such as the
19:706:41407:18:18:71:469:653:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:Great Depression, on the other hand, have important and longlasting effects.
19:707:41483:18:18:89:539:635:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:We therefore turn to the historical record of the United States and study the predicted
19:708:41570:18:18:71:539:617:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:response of fertility to the productivity booms and busts over the 20th century. To do this,
19:709:41662:18:18:71:539:599:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:we must first construct a series of shocks to feed into the model. It is not obvious how to do
19:710:41756:18:18:71:540:581:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:this realistically. On the one hand, in the model, it is assumed that the shock for the current
19:711:41851:18:18:71:539:563:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:period is realized at the beginning of the period. Effectively, this means that individuals
19:712:41942:18:18:71:539:545:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:know, at the beginning of the period, what the sequence of annual shocks will be over the
19:713:42031:18:18:71:540:527:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:next 20 years.  Even with highly correlated shocks at annual frequency, this assumption
19:714:42118:18:18:71:539:509:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:seems extreme at best.  Another alternative is to decrease the length of a period.  This
19:715:42206:18:17:71:540:491:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:necessarily increases the size of the state space. For example, even decreasing the period
19:716:42296:17:18:71:539:474:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:length to 10 years and maintaining thei.i.d.assumption increases the size of the state space
19:717:42388:18:18:72:539:456:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:from 2 dimensional to 5.14Relaxing thei.i.d.assumption which might be required for 10-
19:718:42474:18:18:72:540:438:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:as opposed to 20-year periods, adds another state variable.In addition, one would have
19:719:42560:18:18:72:539:420:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:to address timing of births (between age 20-30 versus 30-40)more seriously.  This is not
19:720:42648:18:18:72:540:402:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:an easy problem (see Caucutt et al. (2002), Doepke et al. (2007) and Sommer (2010) for
19:721:42734:18:18:72:540:384:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:examples). Thus, there are technical difficulties with following the strategy of decreasing the
19:722:42829:18:18:72:143:366:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:period length.
19:723:42843:18:18:89:539:348:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:Because of this, we present results for several alternativemethods for constructing the
19:724:42930:18:18:72:540:330:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:relevant series of productivity shocks for TFP and LP. First, since the data shows that over
19:725:43022:18:18:72:539:312:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:the period 1940 to 1980 at least 60 percent of all births are towomen age 20 to 30,15we assume
19:726:43116:18:18:72:540:294:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:that women have all their children between age 21 and 30. As our baseline experiment, we
19:727:43204:18:18:72:539:276:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:therefore assume that the relevant productivity shock for them is the average one in the data
19:728:43297:18:17:72:540:258:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:for that 10 year period. We conduct sensitivity analysis on this specification in Section 5.1.
19:729:43391:17:18:72:540:241:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:We also have to choose which productivity measure to use, total factor productivity (TFP)
19:730:43480:18:18:72:539:223:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:or labor productivity (LP). We use TFP in our baseline case and discuss LP in Section 5.2.
19:731:43570:18:18:89:540:205:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:In our baseline experiment, a shock to productivity (or labor income) the size of the Great
19:732:43661:18:18:72:539:187:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:Depression gives rise to a contemporary baby bust that accounts for 64 to 103 percent of the
19:733:43753:18:16:81:539:169:7:g_d0_f4:10:g_d0_f5:F:13As will be seen below, the model response to a shock is smallerwhen the cost is in terms of time than
19:734:43856:16:12:71:539:153:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:when it is in terms of goods. Thus, ’between 1 and 1.7’ corresponds to 1 with a time cost and 1.7 with a
19:735:43960:12:8:71:119:141:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:goods cost.
19:736:43971:8:16:81:539:133:7:g_d0_f4:10:g_d0_f5:F:14The state space is three-dimensional in our problem:N1, N2ands. Using the homogeneity results,
19:737:44067:16:11:71:540:117:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:this can be reduced to two state variables,N=N1/N2ands. With 10-year periods (and age-groups) the
19:738:44164:11:12:71:539:106:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:problem becomes six-dimensional:N2b, N1,1, N1,2, N2,1, N2,2ands, which using the same methods can be
19:739:44264:12:9:71:240:94:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:reduced to a five-dimensional problem.
19:740:44302:9:0:81:313:85:7:g_d0_f4:10:g_d0_f5:A:15See Vital Statistics of the United States, Table 1-7.
20:741:44359:0:18:71:540:707:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:reduction in CTFR and 39 to 63 percent of the reduction in TFR seen in the data. Further,
20:742:44448:18:18:71:540:689:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:the model prediction of the lagged response to such a shock isa baby boom. Combined
20:743:44531:18:18:71:540:671:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:with the productivity boom in the 1950s and 1960s, the predicted size of the baby boom lies
20:744:44622:18:18:71:540:653:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:between 84 and 150 percent of the actual size of the baby boom observed in the CTFR data
20:745:44710:18:29:71:373:635:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:and 53 to 92 percent of the one observed in the TFR data.
20:746:44767:29:21:71:229:606:14:g_d0_f1:14:g_d0_f1:C:4.1  Parameterization
20:747:44788:21:18:71:540:585:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f2:B:Preference Parameters:Throughout, we assume thatη= 1−σand setσ= 3 following
20:748:44863:18:18:72:539:567:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:Jones and Schoonbroodt (2010) among others.16The discount factor is set toβ= 0.9620to
20:749:44948:18:18:72:325:549:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:match an annual interest rate of about 4 percent.
20:750:44997:18:17:72:539:531:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f2:B:Wage and Productivity Parameters:From the model, wages are given bywat=stγtwa,
20:751:45075:17:18:72:540:514:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:wherewais the base wage (in period 0) for workers of agea= 1,2 ,γis the trend productivity
20:752:45165:18:18:72:539:496:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:growth rate for a 20-year period andstis the productivity shock which we assume to be
20:753:45250:18:18:72:539:478:12:g_d0_f8:12:g_d0_f2:B:i.i.d.over time.17Further, for computational reasons, it is convenient to assume a functional
20:754:45343:18:18:72:539:460:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:form for the distribution of productivity shocks,st. We assume that ln ˆst∼N(0,σ2s) where
20:755:45432:18:0:72:116:442:12:g_d0_f2:8:g_d0_f9:B:ˆst=steσ
20:756:45440:0:7:116:128:449:6:g_d0_f14:8:g_d0_f3:?:2s/2
20:757:45444:7:18:132:539:442:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:soE(st) = 1. Thus, the parameter values to be determined arew1,w2,γandσs.
20:758:45517:18:18:89:540:424:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:We normalizew1= 1 and choosew2= 1.25.  This is in line with life-cycle earnings
20:759:45596:18:18:72:540:406:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:profiles from Hansen (1993) and Huggett (1996). The growth rate of productivity,γ, and
20:760:45682:18:18:71:539:388:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:the standard deviation of productivity shocks,σs, are calculated from the TFP series plotted
20:761:45774:18:18:71:539:370:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:in Figure 1. First, we compute the linear trend in productivity by running the following
20:762:45862:18:33:71:321:352:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:ordinary least-squares regression on annual data:
20:763:45911:33:32:71:368:319:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f8:B:(10)lnTFPt=α0+α1t+-t.
20:764:45932:32:18:71:539:287:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:We find thatα1= 0.0159 and therefore setγ= (eα1)20= 1.016120. That is, productivity
20:765:46015:18:18:71:423:269:12:g_d0_f2:8:g_d0_f3:B:grows at an average of 1.61 percent per year over the 20th century.18
20:766:46084:18:18:89:539:251:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:To pin down the value ofσsseveral steps are required. First, in our baseline experiment,
20:767:46172:18:18:72:540:233:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:we use the 10 most fertile years of each cohort within a dynasty, namely age 20 to 30, to
20:768:46261:18:18:72:540:215:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:determine the productivity shock this cohort’s fertility choice is affected by. Thus, the shock
20:769:46356:18:0:72:234:197:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:we are interested in is ln ˆst= ln
20:770:46390:0:13:236:262:207:12:g_d0_f11:8:g_d0_f3:?:(∑10
20:771:46394:13:0:254:275:194:8:g_d0_f9:12:g_d0_f8:?:t=1e
20:772:46398:0:7:276:420:201:8:g_d0_f9:8:g_d0_f3:?:-t)−μwhereμ=E(ln(∑10
20:773:46418:7:0:412:433:194:8:g_d0_f9:12:g_d0_f8:?:t=1e
20:774:46422:0:24:434:540:201:8:g_d0_f9:12:g_d0_f2:A:-t)). To approximate
20:775:46442:24:12:81:458:177:7:g_d0_f4:10:g_d0_f5:A:16e.g., Mateos-Planas (2002) and Scholz and Seshadri (2009) also use a value ofσ= 3.
20:776:46526:12:15:81:540:165:7:g_d0_f4:10:g_d0_f5:F:17This is a reasonable approximation for long movements in labor productivity across generations, which
20:777:46629:15:9:72:230:150:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:within a dynasty are 20 years apart.
20:778:46665:9:15:81:539:141:7:g_d0_f4:10:g_d0_f5:F:18An alternative detrending method would be to use a Hodrick-Prescott filter, instead. However, it is
20:779:46766:15:12:72:540:126:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:not clear what value to use for the smoothing parameter sincewe are interested in long fluctuations, rather
20:780:46873:12:12:72:539:114:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:than quarter-to-quarter or annual deviations from trend. In any case, for low enough smoothing parameter
20:781:46977:12:12:72:539:102:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:resulting productivity shocks would typically be smaller.Assuming that the growth rate of productivity is
20:782:47082:12:12:72:539:90:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:constant in the model but using HP productivity shocks wouldresult in smaller fertility responses in the
20:783:47186:12:0:72:298:78:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:experiments at the end of this and the next section.
21:784:47240:0:33:71:493:707:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:its standard deviation,σs, we assume that-tfollows an AR1 process and estimate
21:785:47318:33:33:71:359:674:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f8:B:(11)-t=ρ0+ρ1-t−1+νt,
21:786:47338:33:18:72:539:641:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:whereνt∼N(0,σν), simulate a long series of{-t}, compute a series{lnst}and calculate
21:787:47421:18:18:72:540:623:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:its standard deviation to getσs= 0.07. Of course, the series, lnst, so constructed is not
21:788:47510:18:18:72:540:605:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:exactly normally distributed.  However, for all simulations, the Kolmogorov-Smirnov test
21:789:47598:18:18:72:539:587:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:produces ap-value of about 0.4. This means that, at any reasonable significance level, the
21:790:47688:18:18:72:540:569:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:test would accept the null hypothesis that the distributionof lnstis normal and hence this
21:791:47778:18:17:72:266:551:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:should provide a good approximation.
21:792:47814:17:18:72:539:534:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f2:B:Costs of Children:Given preference and productivity parameters, we then calibrate child
21:793:47901:18:18:72:539:516:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:costs,θ(·),to match an annual population growth rate of 0.645 percent per year (see Haines,
21:794:47992:18:0:72:446:498:12:g_d0_f2:8:g_d0_f9:B:1994, Table 1).  In the model, population growth correspondstoNb+N
21:795:48058:0:12:447:469:506:6:g_d0_f14:6:g_d0_f14:?:1+N2
21:796:48062:12:14:421:539:494:8:g_d0_f9:12:g_d0_f2:A:N1+N2+N3.In a steady
21:797:48082:14:18:71:539:480:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:state with no uncertainty, this is given by the level of fertility choice (per person),nss,
21:798:48173:18:18:71:540:462:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:satisfyingn′(nss,1) =nss.Thus, the target isnss= 1.0064520.This corresponds to a steady
21:799:48260:18:18:71:539:444:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:state CTFR level ofnss/0.5 = 2.27 children per woman. In the goods cost case, we find
21:800:48345:18:18:71:539:426:12:g_d0_f8:12:g_d0_f2:B:θ= 0.1932, while with time costs we findb= 0.1927. Since the wage for young workers is
21:801:48431:18:18:71:540:408:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:normalized to 1, these costs imply that it takes about 20 percent of a young worker’s income
21:802:48523:18:18:71:540:390:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:or time to produce a child. This means that a two person household can at most have ten
21:803:48610:18:18:71:115:372:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:children.
21:804:48619:18:34:71:379:354:12:g_d0_f1:8:g_d0_f3:C:Summary:These parameters are summarized in Table 2.19
21:805:48672:34:27:212:398:320:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:?:Table 2: Parameter Values, Baseline

21:809:48797:73:21:71:284:184:14:g_d0_f1:14:g_d0_f1:C:4.2  Model impulse responses
21:810:48825:21:18:71:539:163:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:Given the parameter values from the previous section we calculate the decision rules from the
21:811:48918:18:18:71:539:145:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:model. These can then be used to estimate the model responsesto different size productivity
21:812:49009:18:17:71:539:127:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:shocks. The results are summarized in Table 3. The rows of thetable correspond to the two
21:813:49098:17:18:71:539:110:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:alternative types of cost structures for children, goods and time. The first two columns report
21:814:49193:18:0:81:419:92:7:g_d0_f4:10:g_d0_f5:A:19We provide sensitivity to the parameter values in the Appendix, Table A.9.
22:815:49271:0:18:71:539:707:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:the elasticities (at the steady state) of (a) current and (b)lagged fertility to productivity
22:816:49364:18:18:71:540:689:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:shocks.  The next two columns give the (c) contemporaneous and (d) lagged change in
22:817:49447:18:18:71:539:671:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:fertility levels that the model predicts will result from a one percent increase in productivity.
22:818:49544:18:36:71:438:653:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:Column (e) recalls the steady state level of completed fertility (CTFR).
22:819:49616:36:34:153:459:617:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:?:Table 3: Impulse Response (in percent and levels), Baseline
22:820:49675:34:29:165:407:583:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:?:Cost% DeviationsCTFR Levels
22:821:49702:29:7:165:191:554:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:?:Type
22:822:49706:7:15:210:446:547:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:?:InitialLaggedInitialLaggedSteady St.
22:823:49742:15:22:218:426:532:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:?:(a)(b)(c)(d)(e)
22:824:49757:22:14:165:433:510:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:?:Goods1.741-1.5612.3142.2392.274
22:825:49788:14:56:165:433:496:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:?:Time1.039-0.9402.2982.2532.274
22:826:49818:56:18:89:539:440:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:As can be seen from this table, in the goods cost case the fertility response to a 1-percent
22:827:49910:18:18:71:539:422:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:productivity shock generates a 1.74 percent contemporaneous increase in fertility (column
22:828:49999:18:18:71:539:404:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:(a)) with a lagged decrease of 1.56 percent one period later (column (b)). In the time cost
22:829:50090:18:18:71:539:386:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:case, the corresponding percentage changes are a 1.04 percent contemporaneous increase and
22:830:50180:18:18:71:236:368:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:a 0.94 decrease one period later.
22:831:50213:18:18:89:539:350:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:These magnitudes can be compared to the regression results shown in Table 1. In par-
22:832:50297:18:18:71:540:332:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:ticular, column (a) is to be compared withλ1and column (b) withλ2. Thus, if the model
22:833:50382:18:18:72:540:314:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:fit the data perfectly we should see 0.83 in column (a), and -0.84 in column (b). Although
22:834:50472:18:18:72:493:296:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:the model quantities are higher, they are remarkably similar in the time cost case.
22:835:50555:18:18:89:540:278:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:Comparing the two cases, the initial effect is 70 percent larger in the goods cost case
22:836:50642:18:18:72:540:260:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:than it is in the time cost case. The reason for this differenceis that when child costs are
22:837:50734:18:18:72:540:242:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:primarily in terms of time there are two offsetting effects. First, when times are good there
22:838:50827:18:18:72:540:224:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:is a natural tendency to save for the future to equalize marginal utilities. Here, the only way
22:839:50922:18:18:72:540:206:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:to do this is by increasing family size. On the other hand, since the cost of raising children is
22:840:51019:18:17:72:539:188:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:positively related to the wage, when times are good, children are relatively more expensive
22:841:51110:17:18:72:540:171:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:– a force in the opposite direction. When the costs of children are in terms of goods, this
22:842:51201:18:18:72:539:153:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:second effect is not at work and hence, the overall effect is larger in this case. As can be seen
22:843:51298:18:18:72:539:135:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:in the table, as the theory predicts, the first effect dominates if the intertemporal elasticity
22:844:51394:18:18:72:360:117:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:of substitution is low enough (σ >1, see Proposition 1).
22:845:51450:18:18:89:539:99:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:Further, since in both cases, the ratio of the initial response to the lagged response is
22:846:51539:18:0:71:540:81:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:about -90 percent, the half-life (in absolute value) of the effect is 7 periods (or generations)
23:847:51637:0:18:71:539:707:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:in both cases. In particular, the effect 7 periods later is a decrease in fertility of 0.8 percent
23:848:51735:18:18:71:539:689:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:in the goods cost case and 0.5 percent in the time cost case (i.e., half of the initial responses).
23:849:51834:18:18:89:539:671:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:To get a sense about the size of these effects note that a one-percent decrease in produc-
23:850:51924:18:18:71:540:653:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:tivity for a 10-year period roughly corresponds to a recession where GDP is 5 percent below
23:851:52015:18:18:71:539:635:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:trend for two years. This would decrease fertility by about 0.02 to 0.04 children per woman
23:852:52106:18:18:71:539:617:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:with a subsequent baby boom of similar size. Thus, the quantitative effects on (completed)
23:853:52196:18:18:71:539:599:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:fertility of a normal sized recession would be quite modest.As we shall see below, the model
23:854:52288:18:29:71:517:581:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:predicts that a recession the size of the Great Depression gives a much larger response.
23:855:52376:29:21:71:446:552:14:g_d0_f1:14:g_d0_f1:C:4.3  A historical episode: the U.S. Great Depression
23:856:52428:21:17:71:539:531:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:In this section we use the historical record of the actual series of productivity shocks in the
23:857:52523:17:18:71:540:514:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:U.S. to study the predicted response of fertility to the productivity busts and booms that
23:858:52613:18:18:71:264:496:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:have occurred over the last 100 years.
23:859:52651:18:18:89:540:478:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:We first focus on the dynasty most affected by the Great Depression, i.e., the cohort
23:860:52736:18:18:71:539:460:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:making fertility decisions in the 1930s (followed by the 1950s cohort, the 1970s cohort and
23:861:52827:18:18:71:163:442:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:the 1990s cohort).
23:862:52845:18:18:89:540:424:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:Second, we consider other dynasties also affected by the Great Depression but less so.
23:863:52931:18:18:71:540:406:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:That is, those making fertility choices either in the late 1920s and early 1930s (i.e., between
23:864:53026:18:18:71:539:388:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:1926 and 1935, etc.) or in the late 1930s and early 1940s (between 1936 and 1945, etc.).
23:865:53114:18:18:71:540:370:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:Finally, we consider the dynasty making fertility choices in the 1920s (between 1921 and
23:866:53202:18:18:71:540:352:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:1930, etc). By construction, the Great Depression has no effect on the fertility choices of
23:867:53293:18:18:71:539:334:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:this last dynasty. Thus, we simplify by assuming that there is no connection or opportunity
23:868:53384:18:18:71:540:316:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:for trade between the four dynasties.  Cohorts within these dynasties make overlapping
23:869:53470:18:17:71:161:298:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:fertility decisions.
23:870:53490:17:18:89:540:281:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:Finally, the measure of fertility constructed in this way – using different shocks for dif-
23:871:53581:18:18:71:540:263:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:ferent cohorts – corresponds to CTFR since it represents completed fertility for each cohort.
23:872:53674:18:18:71:539:245:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:We also construct an analog for TFR, i.e., period fertility,by averaging over the two cohorts
23:873:53767:18:18:71:540:227:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:making their fertility decisions in any five-year period. Indoing this, we solve the model
23:874:53857:18:25:71:364:209:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:separately for the different dynasties and then aggregate.
23:875:53915:25:21:71:354:184:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:C:4.3.1  CTFR: Most Affected Dynasty (M.A.D.)
23:876:53958:21:17:71:539:163:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:First, to focus on the effects of the Great Depression, we start with the dynasty making
23:877:54046:17:18:71:539:146:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:fertility decisions in the 1910s, 1930s, 1950s, 1970s and 1990s. This dynasty is the most
23:878:54135:18:18:71:539:128:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:affected by the Great Depression. As a benchmark, we assume that the initial state of the
23:879:54224:18:18:71:539:110:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:dynasty is at its steady state age-distribution. In other words, the dynasty has been facing
23:880:54316:18:0:71:517:92:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:average shocks (s= 1) for a long time so that its current age-distribution is stationary.
24:881:54407:0:18:89:540:707:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:For this dynasty, the relevant series of productivity shocks,st, is shown in Table 4.
24:882:54492:18:18:71:539:689:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:The results are shown in Figure 4. With a goods cost, the modelpredicts that fertility is
24:883:54581:18:18:71:540:671:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:19.1 percent below trend during the 1930s; with a time cost the prediction is 11.8 percent
24:884:54671:18:18:71:539:653:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:below trend. In the data, CTFR actually fell by 18.5 percent.Thus, the model captures
24:885:54756:18:18:71:539:635:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:a significant fraction of this movement in either case. This is primarily due to the large,
24:886:54847:18:18:71:539:617:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:negative shock of 12.4 percent during the Great Depression of the 1930s – the dynasty’s
24:887:54934:18:18:71:539:599:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:young-to-old worker ratio is initially almost at its steadystate value and hence effects from
24:888:55027:18:33:71:158:581:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:this are minimal.

24:905:55338:7:10:231:299:405:8:Helvetica:8:Helvetica:?:Model: Goods cost
24:906:55355:10:9:231:293:395:8:Helvetica:8:Helvetica:?:Model: Time cost
24:907:55371:9:9:231:272:386:8:Helvetica:8:Helvetica:?:CTFR: Data
24:908:55381:9:192:231:297:377:8:Helvetica:8:Helvetica:?:Cohort TFP Shock
24:909:55397:192:18:89:539:185:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:As noted above, there is also a lagged effect in the model from the Great Depression due
24:910:55485:18:18:71:539:167:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:to the misalignment of the age structure that results from low fertility during the 1930s.
24:911:55575:18:17:71:539:149:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:This implies, a baby boom for the next cohort in this dynasty which takes place in the
24:912:55661:17:18:71:539:132:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:1950s. With a goods cost, the model prediction is an increasein fertility above trend of 34.9
24:913:55754:18:18:71:539:114:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:percent, while with a time cost, the corresponding model prediction is 19.7 percent above
24:914:55843:18:18:71:540:96:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:trend. These model predictions result from a combination ofthe fertility response to the
24:915:55931:18:0:71:540:78:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:low young-to-old worker ratio due to the baby bust and the 8.4percent productivity boom
25:916:56020:0:18:71:539:707:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:in the 1950s. For the purposes of comparison, the peak of the baby boom in the CTFR data
25:917:56108:18:18:71:204:689:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:is 23 percent above trend.
25:918:56134:18:18:89:539:671:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:In the model, the Great Depression has continuing impacts onthe fertility choices in this
25:919:56223:18:18:71:539:653:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:dynasty – there are continued fluctuations with a decrease of11 to 18 percent below trend
25:920:56312:18:18:71:540:635:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:in the 1970s (due to the lagged response to the baby boom but dampened by the 3.9 percent
25:921:56401:18:18:71:539:617:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:upward fluctuation in TFP in the 1970s); this is followed by anincrease of 2.7 to 4.2 percent
25:922:56494:18:18:71:539:599:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:above trend in the 1990s – again a response to the low young-to-old worker ratio dampened
25:923:56583:18:18:71:407:581:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:by the negative productivity shock of 8 percent during the 1990s.
25:924:56648:18:18:89:539:563:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:This is an extreme version of the baby bust and boom in the model because it is con-
25:925:56732:18:18:71:539:545:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:structed so that the cohort’s entire fertile period falls during the Great Depression. For other
25:926:56828:18:18:71:539:527:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:cohorts or dynasties, the effects are mitigated because at least part of their fertile period
25:927:56921:18:18:71:539:509:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:is unaffected by the Great Depression. This is also true for the baby boom: the dynasty
25:928:57008:18:17:71:539:491:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:considered here is most affected by the low young-to-old worker ratio and has a large positive
25:929:57102:17:25:71:288:474:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:productivity shock in the post-war period.
25:930:57144:25:21:71:308:449:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:C:4.3.2  CTFR: All dynasties and cohorts
25:931:57182:21:18:71:539:428:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:Table 5 shows how we construct our four dynasties. It illustrates the state variables and
25:932:57271:18:18:71:539:410:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:shocks for each cohort.  For each dynasty, we assume that the initial value of the state
25:933:57359:18:18:71:539:392:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:variable isn=nss,the balanced growth level of fertility. The first dynasty (d1) starts with
25:934:57450:18:18:72:540:374:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:women born between 1876-1880 (labeled 1880), cohort 1 (c1).  They face a productivity
25:935:57535:18:17:71:539:356:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:shock,s1,averaging over the period 1901-1910 when they are roughly age 20 to 30. Their
25:936:57621:17:18:71:539:339:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:fertility choice isnc1=n′(nss,s1).The second dynasty (d2) starts with women born five
25:937:57706:18:18:71:539:321:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:years later, between 1881-1885 (labeled 1885), cohort 2 (c2). Their first productivity shock,
25:938:57799:18:18:71:539:303:12:g_d0_f8:12:g_d0_f2:B:s2,is obtained by averaging over 1906-15. Their fertility choice isnc2=n′(nss,s2).The
25:939:57884:18:18:71:540:285:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:third dynasty (d3) starts with women born between 1886-1890 (labeled 1890), cohort 3 (c3).
25:940:57974:18:18:71:540:267:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:This is the most affected dynasty described above.  The fourthdynasty (d4) starts with
25:941:58060:18:18:71:539:249:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:women born between 1891-1895 (labeled 1895), cohort 4 (c4). Their first productivity shock
25:942:58150:18:18:71:540:231:12:g_d0_f8:12:g_d0_f2:B:s4,comes from averaging over the period 1916-1925.  Cohort 5, born between 1896-1900
25:943:58234:18:18:71:540:213:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:(labeled 1900) are the next generation ind1. Their initial productivity shocks5,comes from
25:944:58324:18:18:71:539:195:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f8:B:averaging over the period 1921-30. Thus, their fertility choice is given bync5=n′(nc1,s5).
25:945:58414:18:18:71:540:177:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:Proceeding further in this fashion leads to Cohort 9, born between 1916-1920 (labeled 1920),
25:946:58506:18:18:71:539:159:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:the third generation in dynasty 1 making fertility decisions  between 1941 and 1950. The
25:947:58594:18:18:71:540:141:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:shock that this cohort faces is not affected by the Great Depression at all. We expand this
25:948:58685:18:17:71:539:123:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:series to Cohort 20, born between 1971-1975 (labeled 1975),the third generation in dynasty
25:949:58775:17:18:71:335:106:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:1 making fertility decisions between 1996 and 2005.

27:997:60720:25:20:71:140:373:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:C:4.3.3  TFR
27:998:60730:20:18:71:539:353:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:Next, we compute an analog of TFR, i.e., period fertility, byaveraging over the two cohorts
27:999:60821:18:18:71:539:335:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:making their fertility decisions in any five-year period. That is, we compute TFR in any
27:1000:60909:18:18:71:539:317:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:five-year period by computing the average of fertility of thetwo cohorts who are making
27:1001:60996:18:18:71:540:299:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:half their fertility decision in that five-year period, weighted by their size (i.e., their parent’s
27:1002:61096:18:7:71:327:281:12:g_d0_f2:6:g_d0_f16:B:fertility choice). For example,TFR1931−35=nc6nc2+nc7nc3nc
27:1003:61153:7:0:328:351:274:6:g_d0_f14:6:g_d0_f14:?:2+nc3
27:1004:61158:0:18:366:540:281:12:g_d0_f8:12:g_d0_f2:A:.Note that, if all dynasties are in
27:1005:61193:18:18:71:540:263:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:a steady stateCTFR=TFR=nss/0.5.One advantage of this fertility measure is that we
27:1006:61274:18:18:71:540:245:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:have data until 2000, while for completed fertility the lastcohort in Jones and Tertilt (2008)
27:1007:61368:18:18:71:262:227:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:makes fertility decisions in the 1980s.
27:1008:61407:18:18:89:539:209:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:The results are shown in Figure 6. Since deviations in TFR in the data are larger than
27:1009:61493:18:18:71:539:191:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:deviations in CTFR and since we are averaging over less affected cohorts, the model accounts
27:1010:61585:18:18:71:280:173:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:for a smaller fraction of these deviations.
27:1011:61628:18:17:89:539:155:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:With a goods cost, the model predicts that TFR would be 12.7 percent below trend in
27:1012:61711:17:18:71:540:138:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:the early and late 1930s; with a time cost, the analog is 7.8 percent. In the data, TFR is
27:1013:61801:18:18:71:539:120:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:20 percent below trend in the early 1930s and 22 percent belowtrend in the late 1930s. As
27:1014:61890:18:18:71:540:102:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:with CTFR, this result is mainly due to the negative shock of 12.4 percent during the Great
27:1015:61981:18:0:71:539:84:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:Depression but here the results are mitigated because some women don’t make their entire
28:1016:62071:0:189:186:425:700:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:?:Figure 6: Percent Deviations in TFR, Baseline
