28:1033:62246:189:18:71:539:453:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:fertility decision during this period. In the data, the peakof the baby boom occurs in the
28:1034:62336:18:18:71:539:435:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:late 1950s when fertility is 31 percent above trend. With a goods cost, the model predicts
28:1035:62426:18:18:71:539:417:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:TFR will be 28.5 percent above trend while with a time cost, the prediction is 16.4 percent.
28:1036:62518:18:18:71:539:399:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:Again, this result is a combination of the fertility response to the low young-to-old worker
28:1037:62610:18:17:71:539:381:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:ratio due to the baby bust and the productivity boom in the 1950s and early 1960s. Hence,
28:1038:62699:17:18:71:540:364:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:the model accounts for 38.5 to 62.6 percent of the baby bust and for 53 to 92 percent of the
28:1039:62791:18:18:71:174:346:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:baby boom in TFR.
28:1040:62808:18:18:89:540:328:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:In subsequent periods, TFR in the model keeps fluctuating – 11.9 to 18.9 percent below
28:1041:62894:18:18:71:539:310:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:trend in the late 1970s (due to the prior baby boom but dampened by the 3.9 percent increase
28:1042:62986:18:18:71:539:292:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:in TFP). The corresponding movements in the data on TFR is 20.7 percent below trend.
28:1043:63070:18:18:71:540:274:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:The increase in fertility in the 1990s of 7.1 percent above trend in the data is also partly
28:1044:63162:18:35:71:416:256:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:captured by the model which predicts a 2.8 to 4.7 percent increase.
28:1045:63229:35:25:71:189:221:17:g_d0_f1:17:g_d0_f1:C:5  Sensitivity
28:1046:63243:25:18:71:540:196:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:In this section, we discuss the sensitivity of our results two types of changes.  The first
28:1047:63334:18:18:71:540:178:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:type take the parameters in Table 2 as given but change the wayproductivity shocks in
28:1048:63419:18:18:71:539:160:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:Section 4.3 are computed. Here, we explore (1) changes in theage range used to assign TFP
28:1049:63508:18:18:71:539:142:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:shocks to different cohorts, and (2) the effects of using laborproductivity (LP) instead of
28:1050:63599:18:18:71:539:124:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:TFP. Given the nature of these experiments, elasticities and impulse responses remain the
28:1051:63688:18:18:71:539:106:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:same but the size of shocks differs. Results are reported in Figure 7. Increasing the age
28:1052:63777:18:0:71:540:88:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:range tends to decrease the effect of the Great Depression on fertility because it is diluted
29:1053:63872:0:18:71:539:707:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:across more cohorts.  Therefore, the predicted size of the baby boom is also smaller.  It
29:1054:63961:18:18:71:540:689:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:also affects the timing of the baby bust and boom. The results using labor productivity
29:1055:64048:18:18:71:540:671:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:are very similar to the baseline, except for the cohort making fertility decisions in the 1910s
29:1056:64143:18:18:71:540:653:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:who experience a productivity boom and those making fertility decisions in the 1920s who
29:1057:64231:18:18:71:539:635:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:experience a significant negative shock. Due to endogenous fluctuations, these changes work
29:1058:64322:18:18:71:418:617:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:themselves through the fertility fluctuations later on in the century.
29:1059:64392:18:18:89:539:599:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:The second type of change that we explore involves altering some of the basic features of
29:1060:64481:18:18:71:540:581:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:the model. Here we study the robustness of our results both toincluding capital and having
29:1061:64571:18:18:71:540:563:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:more (i.e., shorter) time periods and hence, more active generations. We find that our basic
29:1062:64663:18:29:71:401:545:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:results are robust to these two qualitative changes to the model.
29:1063:64728:29:20:71:342:516:14:g_d0_f1:14:g_d0_f1:C:5.1  Age range of productivity shocks
29:1064:64765:20:18:71:539:496:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:One problem with the assumption that the only relevant shockfor the dynasty is when their
29:1065:64854:18:18:71:539:478:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:youngest cohort is age 20 to 30 is that it implicitly assumes that the shock lasts for the entire
29:1066:64951:18:18:71:539:460:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:period of 20 years. In this section, we describe model results when the shock is set to the
29:1067:65042:18:18:71:463:442:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:average productivity shock over the period where the cohortis age 20 to 40.
29:1068:65117:18:18:89:539:424:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:Figure 7(a), shows cohort productivity shocks for this experiment compared to the base-
29:1069:65204:18:18:71:539:406:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:line. Overall shocks are smoother and smaller because some busts and booms are smoothed
29:1070:65291:18:18:71:539:388:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:out due to the longer time period. Also, Cohortc5making part of their fertility decisions in
29:1071:65383:18:18:72:539:370:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:the 1920s faces a relatively large negative shock due to the Great Depression in the 1930s
29:1072:65473:18:18:72:539:352:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:whenc5is age 30 to 40. Therefore, the baby bust starts earlier and issmaller than in the
29:1073:65561:18:18:72:540:334:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:baseline model as can be seen in Figure 7(b). Because of the smaller baby bust in the 1930s
29:1074:65652:18:18:72:511:316:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:and smaller shocks, the baby boom is also smaller and so are subsequent fluctuations.
29:1075:65737:18:17:89:540:298:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:The problem with this alternative is that it implicitly assumes that fertility is uniform
29:1076:65826:17:18:72:540:281:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:between age 20 and 40. Hence, the fertility decision of womenborn at the beginning of the
29:1077:65915:18:18:72:540:263:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:century is heavily affected by the Great Depression in this experiment when in reality most
29:1078:66006:18:18:72:329:245:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:of them had completed their fertility in the 1920s.
29:1079:66057:18:18:89:539:227:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:Since this alternative is at the opposite extreme of the baseline, the two experiments
29:1080:66143:18:18:72:539:209:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:together should give us reasonable bounds on how much of the fluctuations in fertility over
29:1081:66234:18:29:72:365:191:12:g_d0_f2:8:g_d0_f3:B:the 20th century in the U.S. the model can account for.20
29:1082:66291:29:21:72:469:162:14:g_d0_f1:14:g_d0_f1:C:5.2  Labor productivity versus total factor productivity
29:1083:66347:21:18:72:539:141:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:Since there is no capital in our model, it is not clear wether we should use labor productivity
29:1084:66442:18:18:72:539:123:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:(LP) or total factor productivity (TFP) to infer productivity shocks. The argument for using
29:1085:66534:18:16:81:539:105:7:g_d0_f4:10:g_d0_f5:F:20In Appendix A.2 we perform an experiment where TFP shocks areweighted by average age-specific
29:1086:66629:16:0:71:477:89:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:fertility rates. This is an intermediate case between the baseline and the present experiment.

30:1138:67241:323:9:71:539:311:8:g_d0_f3:8:g_d0_f3:F:Note: Tables A.1 to A.3 in Appendix A.1 show the exact numbersof shocks, CTFR and TFR responses for the two experiments
30:1139:67360:9:34:71:166:302:8:g_d0_f3:8:g_d0_f3:F:compared to the baseline.
30:1140:67385:34:18:71:540:268:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:TFP is that if capital were included in the model, fertility would respond to TFP alongside
30:1141:67476:18:18:71:539:250:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:investment. The argument for using LP is that most people don’t own much capital (besides
30:1142:67565:18:18:71:469:232:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:durable goods and houses) so that their fertility response is to changes in LP.
30:1143:67644:18:17:89:539:214:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:Here we perform the same experiment as in the main text but usedeviations from trend
30:1144:67728:17:18:71:539:197:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:in LP instead of TFP. Again, we fit a linear trend through the LPdata.  The resulting
30:1145:67813:18:18:71:539:179:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:productivity shocks for each cohort are compared to the baseline in Figure 7(c). As can be
30:1146:67903:18:18:71:539:161:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:seen in the figure, there are two main differences between the LP compared to the TFP series
30:1147:67995:18:18:71:540:143:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:of shocks. The very first cohort making fertility decisions in the early 1900s experience a
30:1148:68086:18:18:71:539:125:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:productivity boom and those making fertility decisions in the 1910s and 1920s experience a
30:1149:68176:18:18:71:539:107:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:significant negative shock. Therefore, as shown in Figure 7(d), the baby bust is already well
30:1150:68269:18:0:71:539:89:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:underway at this time. Despite the similar size of the shock in the 1930s, fertility decreases
31:1151:68365:0:18:71:540:707:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:less in the LP case than in the TFP case. This is because of the mitigating effect of the low
31:1152:68458:18:18:71:539:689:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:young-to-old worker ration due to low fertility in the 1910s. Subsequently, the baby boom
31:1153:68547:18:18:71:539:671:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:starts earlier but is of similar size as the baseline due to the larger LP shock in the 1950s
31:1154:68640:18:18:71:460:653:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:and 1960s compared to TFP. Overall, however, the results arequite similar.
31:1155:68714:18:18:89:539:635:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:Note that the growth rate of LP in the data is 2.23 percent per year on average, while
31:1156:68800:18:18:71:539:617:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:it is only 1.61 percent per year for TFP. Also, the estimated standard deviation of shocks
31:1157:68890:18:18:71:539:599:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:is 0.08 in most simulations while it is only 0.07 for TFP. In the experiment above, we held
31:1158:68981:18:18:71:539:581:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:these two parameters constant so that we perform only one change at a time and this may
31:1159:69068:18:29:71:245:563:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:have some effect on the outcomes.
31:1160:69101:29:20:71:387:534:14:g_d0_f1:14:g_d0_f1:C:5.3  The effects of including physical capital

32:1253:71998:29:21:72:363:199:14:g_d0_f1:14:g_d0_f1:C:5.4  Three Period Working Life (3PWL)
32:1254:72035:21:17:72:539:178:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:While the initial fertility response to productivity shocks is very similar whetherw2>0 or

32:1256:72207:18:16:81:539:143:7:g_d0_f4:10:g_d0_f5:F:21One can show show that in a model with stochastic ageing (useful to obtain reasonable working life
32:1257:72307:16:12:71:540:127:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:lengths while keeping the state space small), the elasticity is larger (smaller) than 1 if the adult survival
32:1258:72416:12:12:71:540:115:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:rate is larger (smaller) than (1−δ).  Since in the present model, survival in the workforce is zero, this
32:1259:72521:12:12:71:540:103:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:is an extreme special case. Thus, introducing capital in general does not change the fertility response to

34:1345:75641:18:18:71:190:310:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:fertility are ambiguous.
34:1346:75665:18:18:89:539:292:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:The simulation consists of off balanced growth path dynamicstaking the fertility devia-
34:1347:75752:18:18:71:539:274:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:tion in and around the 1930s from the baseline as given. That is, everything plotted until
34:1348:75842:18:18:71:539:256:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:1940 is exogenous here and we are interested in the baby boom and the potential for damp-
34:1349:75931:18:18:71:539:238:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:ened oscillations thereafter. Note that in the deterministic version of the model, whether
34:1350:76021:18:18:71:539:220:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:costs are in terms of time or goods is irrelevant – the only reason why the two cases would
34:1351:76112:18:18:71:534:202:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:differ here is that we use different baby busts from the baseline results as initial conditions.
34:1352:76208:18:18:89:539:184:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:Figure 8 showsCTFRfor all cohorts for goods costs. As can be seen, magnitudes don’t
34:1353:76291:18:18:71:539:166:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:differ much between the deterministic model with two versus three periods of working life.
34:1354:76381:18:18:71:540:148:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:Hence, most of the difference to the baseline results are due to the lack of additional pro-
34:1355:76473:18:17:71:539:130:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:ductivity shocks, reinforcing the baby boom (and hence subsequent fluctuations). However,
34:1356:76562:17:18:71:539:113:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:the baby boom happens slightly earlier. This is less of an issue of length of the period as
34:1357:76653:18:0:71:540:95:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:it is one of length of generations. In the three period model,parents and children are 15,
35:1358:76745:0:18:71:540:707:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:instead of 20 years apart. The analogous results forCTFRwith a time cost andTFRwith
35:1359:76828:18:18:72:410:689:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:both goods and time costs are similar and hence are not included.
35:1360:76893:18:18:89:540:671:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:In sum, the dampened oscillations we find in the baseline model are not just an artifact
35:1361:76981:18:35:72:249:653:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:of having two periods working life.
35:1362:77016:35:25:72:294:618:17:g_d0_f1:17:g_d0_f1:C:6  International Evidence
35:1363:77041:25:18:72:539:593:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:In this section, we show that countries with a smaller Depression in the 1930s also tend
35:1364:77129:18:18:72:540:575:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:to have smaller baby busts and that those with smaller baby busts tend to have smaller
35:1365:77215:18:18:72:539:557:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:baby booms in the 1950s, conditional on the economic circumstances in the 1950s. Before
35:1366:77302:18:18:72:540:539:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:we start, several data related issues should be addressed. These relate to data availability,
35:1367:77395:18:18:72:330:521:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:detrending methods and dealing with war periods.
35:1368:77443:18:18:89:539:503:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:Ideally, we would like to use the same measures of fertility and productivity as we do for
35:1369:77533:18:18:72:539:485:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:the U.S.. However, for the 20th century complete TFR series and consistent TFP measures
35:1370:77620:18:18:72:540:467:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:are only available for few countries (see e.g., Chesnais (1992) for TFR and Cette et al. (2009)
35:1371:77715:18:18:72:540:449:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:for TFP). We therefore use Crude Birth Rates (CBR) mostly from Mitchell (1998) and GDP
35:1372:77801:18:17:72:540:431:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:per capita from Barro and Ursua (2008), instead.22This gives us 17 countries for which we
35:1373:77890:17:18:72:242:414:12:g_d0_f2:8:g_d0_f3:B:have data from 1900 until 1993.23
35:1374:77923:18:18:89:540:396:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:As to detrending, for CBR, we use the same detrending method as we did for TFR in the
35:1375:78008:18:18:72:540:378:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:U.S., namely a Hodrick-Prescott (HP) filter. While for the U.S. a linear detrending method
35:1376:78098:18:18:72:540:360:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:for log-TFP poses few problems, this is not true for all countries we consider. In particular,
35:1377:78192:18:18:72:539:342:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:if one uses linear detrending, countries such as Portugal orJapan show a permanent negative
35:1378:78283:18:18:72:539:324:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:deviation in GDP early in the century and a permanent positive one later on. Besides the
35:1379:78371:18:18:72:458:306:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:linear detrending method, we therefore also use an HP filter in comparison.
35:1380:78446:18:18:89:539:288:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:Furthermore, the two world wars affected fertility levels inseveral ways that are unrelated
35:1381:78537:18:18:72:539:270:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:to our mechanism. For example, men being away to fight prevents some women from having
35:1382:78623:18:18:72:540:252:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:children. Typically, there is rapid catching up as soon as the men return, if they do (e.g.,
35:1383:78715:18:18:72:539:234:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:see the period 1943-1946 in U.S. data shown in Figure 3). The longer the war involvement
35:1384:78803:18:18:72:540:216:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:and the larger the death rate of soldiers, the more unpredictable this effect on fertility rates
35:1385:78899:18:17:72:539:198:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:becomes.  Similar issues emerge for GDP data during war periods. For example, a large
35:1386:78984:17:16:81:540:181:7:g_d0_f4:10:g_d0_f5:F:22CBR data form 1900 to 1993 are obtained from Mitchell (1998) for all countries, except Germany.
35:1387:79081:16:12:71:540:165:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:German data was combined using Chesnais (1992, Appendix A1)for the 1900 to 1943 period and Statistisches
35:1388:79185:12:12:71:540:153:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:Bundesamt (2007) for 1946 to 1993, linearly interpolating 1944 and 1945. All GDP per capita data comes
35:1389:79287:12:8:71:462:141:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:from Barro and Ursua (2008) and is in millions 1990 International Geary-Khamis dollars.
35:1390:79374:8:16:81:539:133:7:g_d0_f4:10:g_d0_f5:F:23The main issues are. CBR as opposed to TFR is sensitive to mortality in certain groups of the population
35:1391:79480:16:12:71:539:117:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:such as wars which affect total population but not the number of fertile women. GDP per capita as opposed
35:1392:79585:12:12:71:539:105:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:to TFP or LP is only an indirect measure of the productivity ofthe labor force and therefore corresponds
35:1393:79689:12:0:71:169:93:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:less well to our model.
36:1394:79714:0:18:71:539:707:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:part of the increase in output in Germany during the war was not for private consumption
36:1395:79802:18:18:71:540:689:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:but rather war related. We therefore also use dummies for warand immediately after war
36:1396:79888:18:18:71:540:671:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:periods to adjust our measures of deviations in CBR and GDP incomparison to deviations
36:1397:79974:18:18:71:356:653:12:g_d0_f2:8:g_d0_f3:B:resulting from a simple linear trend and the HP filter.24
36:1398:80031:18:18:89:539:635:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:Given these provisos, we are now ready to analyze sizes of depressions, baby busts and
36:1399:80117:18:18:71:539:617:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:baby booms. First, we aim to determine the correlation between the size of the Depression
36:1400:80206:18:18:71:540:599:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:in the 1930s and the size of the baby bust at the time across countries. Since the timing of
36:1401:80298:18:18:71:540:581:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:the Depression was different across countries, we divide the1930s into two periods: 1930 to
36:1402:80389:18:18:71:539:563:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:1934 and 1935 to 1939. We then run eight cross-country OLS regressions, one for each time
36:1403:80478:18:18:71:539:545:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:period combined with one of four detrending methods. Let̂Xitdenote the percent deviation
36:1404:80566:18:30:71:371:527:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f8:B:from trend in variableXin countryiin five-year periodt.
36:1405:80621:30:0:233:258:497:12:g_d0_f12:12:g_d0_f8:?:̂CBR
36:1406:80625:0:12:258:261:504:8:g_d0_f9:8:g_d0_f9:?:i
36:1407:80626:12:0:258:353:492:8:g_d0_f9:12:g_d0_f8:?:t=λ1,t+λ2,t̂GDP
36:1408:80641:0:12:354:357:504:8:g_d0_f9:8:g_d0_f9:?:i
36:1409:80642:12:0:354:378:492:8:g_d0_f9:12:g_d0_f8:?:t+ε
36:1410:80645:0:38:378:381:500:8:g_d0_f9:8:g_d0_f9:?:i
36:1411:80646:38:18:72:539:462:12:g_d0_f2:8:g_d0_f3:B:wheret={30−34,35−39}.The results for each detrending method are given in Table 6.25
36:1412:80729:18:18:89:539:444:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:As can be seen, the coefficient,λ2,t, is positive for all detrending methods and both time
36:1413:80819:18:18:72:540:426:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:periods. It is larger (with a lowerp-value) whenever an HP filter is used rather than linear
36:1414:80911:18:18:72:540:408:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:detrending and whenever the effects of wars are taken into account in detrending. Also, for
36:1415:81002:18:18:72:493:390:12:g_d0_f2:8:g_d0_f3:B:most detrending methods,λ2,tis larger in the early 1930s than in the late 1930s.26
36:1416:81084:18:18:89:539:372:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:Second, we aim to determine whether a larger baby bust in the 1930s implies a larger
36:1417:81168:18:18:72:540:354:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:baby boom in the 1950s, controlling for the GDP deviation in the 1950s. To do this, we
36:1418:81254:18:18:72:540:336:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:run eight cross-country OLS regressions, one for each time period combined with one of four
36:1419:81345:18:25:72:177:318:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:detrending methods.
36:1420:81364:25:0:194:219:293:12:g_d0_f12:12:g_d0_f8:?:̂CBR
36:1421:81368:0:12:219:222:300:8:g_d0_f9:8:g_d0_f9:?:i
36:1422:81369:12:0:219:315:288:8:g_d0_f9:12:g_d0_f8:?:t=λ3,t+λ4,t̂CBR
36:1423:81384:0:12:314:317:300:8:g_d0_f9:8:g_d0_f9:?:i
36:1424:81385:12:0:314:391:288:8:g_d0_f9:12:g_d0_f8:?:t−20+λ5,t̂GDP
36:1425:81398:0:12:393:396:300:8:g_d0_f9:8:g_d0_f9:?:i
36:1426:81399:12:0:393:417:288:8:g_d0_f9:12:g_d0_f8:?:t+ε
36:1427:81402:0:38:416:419:296:8:g_d0_f9:8:g_d0_f9:A:i
36:1428:81403:38:18:72:539:258:12:g_d0_f2:8:g_d0_f3:B:wheret={50−54,55−59}.The results for each detrending method are given in Table 7.27
36:1429:81486:18:18:89:539:240:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:As can be seen, the coefficient,λ4,t, is negative for all detrending methods and both
36:1430:81571:18:18:72:540:222:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:time periods. It is larger in absolute value (with a lowerp-value) whenever an HP filter
36:1431:81659:18:18:72:539:204:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:is used rather than linear detrending and, for the late 1950s, whenever the effects of wars
36:1432:81750:18:19:72:540:186:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:are taken into account in detrending. Also,λ4,tis larger in the early 1930s than in the late
36:1433:81842:19:12:81:335:167:7:g_d0_f4:10:g_d0_f5:A:24For details on detrending procedures, see Appendix A.3.

36:1435:82002:15:9:71:440:140:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:trend inCBRandGDPin the 1930s for each country and each detrending method.

38:1480:83668:18:18:89:539:653:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:In sum then, international evidence on the sizes of depressions and baby busts in the
38:1481:83753:18:18:72:540:635:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:1930s strongly supports our theory while the evidence for the size of the subsequent baby
38:1482:83842:18:40:72:329:617:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:boom, though rightly signed, is somewhat weaker.
38:1483:83890:40:25:72:271:577:17:g_d0_f1:17:g_d0_f1:C:7  Concluding remarks
38:1484:83911:25:18:72:540:552:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:In this paper, we have studied the properties of models of fertility choice with dynastic al-
38:1485:84004:18:18:72:540:534:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:truism in which both aggregate shocks and multiple generations are present. We have shown
38:1486:84093:18:18:72:540:516:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:that in simple versions of this class of models, fertility isprocyclical and has dampened
38:1487:84182:18:18:72:540:498:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:oscillations when perturbed away from the steady state. Moreover, these effects are quan-
38:1488:84271:18:18:72:540:480:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:titatively significant. In calibrated simulations of the model, we find that in response to a
38:1489:84364:18:18:72:540:462:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:negative shock to income like the Great Depression, the decline in fertility in the model is
38:1490:84456:18:17:72:540:444:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:between 40 and 70 percent of the decline actually seen in the data. Moreover, because of a
38:1491:84546:17:18:72:539:427:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:(mild) productivity boom in the 1950s and early 1960s in conjunction with the oscillatory
38:1492:84635:18:18:72:539:409:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:effects discussed above, the model accounts for between 60 and 90 percent of the Baby Boom.
38:1493:84726:18:18:72:539:391:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:The key parameter determining the quantitative findings is the nature of the cost of children
38:1494:84819:18:280:72:176:373:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:– goods versus time.
38:1495:84839:280:15:81:540:93:7:g_d0_f4:10:g_d0_f5:F:28Observations relating to busts and booms across countries leading to these regression results are given
38:1496:84944:15:0:71:154:78:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:in Appendix A.3.1.

45:1656:93218:0:26:71:433:707:14:g_d0_f1:14:g_d0_f1:C:A.2  Additional experiment relating to Section 5.1
45:1657:93268:26:18:71:540:681:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:A second alternative is to use the average productivity shock ages 15 to 45, weighted by the
45:1658:93360:18:18:71:539:663:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:percentage of all births born to the particular age-groups.This takes the typical timing of
45:1659:93451:18:18:71:539:645:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:births into account. In addition, even if the shock we attribute to any individual cohort does
45:1660:93545:18:18:71:540:627:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:not correspond to the actual 20 year shock they experienced,it leaves the aggregate shock
45:1661:93634:18:18:71:540:609:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:in any five year period at its correct level (since different dynasties receive a fraction of it).
45:1662:93732:18:35:71:540:591:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:To see this, consider Table A.4. Now, consider a cohort that is age 15-19 in 1901-05. The
45:1663:93821:35:27:86:525:556:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:Table A.4: Percent of total births that occur to five-year agegroup (average 1940-70)
45:1664:93906:27:14:88:523:529:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:Age group1: 15-192: 20-243: 25-294: 30-345: 35-396: 40-447: 45-49
45:1665:93971:14:57:88:523:515:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:% all births12.4532.8427.1916.548.402.390.18
45:1666:94015:57:18:71:539:458:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:seven relevant shocks are from 1901-05 to 1931-35. However,the weight of the shock in the
45:1667:94104:18:18:71:539:440:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:beginning of the Great Depression, 1931-35, will only receive weight 0.0018. The next cohort
45:1668:94196:18:18:71:539:422:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:of women, age 15-19 in 1906-10 will receive a weight of 0.0239for the same shock and so on.
45:1669:94287:18:18:71:540:404:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:Finally, the cohort age 15-19 in 1931-35 will receive a weight 0.1245 for this shock. Hence,
45:1670:94379:18:18:71:539:386:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:100 percent of the Great Depression shock will be accounted for as part of 7 distinct cohort
45:1671:94471:18:18:71:540:368:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:shocks. The results from this experiment can be seen in Figure A.1. The results are quite
45:1672:94560:18:17:71:522:350:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:similar, albeit smoother and somewhat smaller, to the baseline described in Section 4.3.
45:1673:94648:17:18:89:540:333:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:Note, however, that this alternative does not allow our cohorts to adjust their timing
45:1674:94734:18:18:71:539:315:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:of births to shocks experienced at different ages while fertile. As shown in Doepke et al.
45:1675:94824:18:18:71:540:297:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:(2007), timing of births for any given cohort did change during the bust-boom-bust episode,
45:1676:94915:18:18:71:539:279:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:albeit to a limited extent. As mentioned before, decreasingthe length of the period in order
45:1677:95007:18:18:71:540:261:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:to start addressing this issue would significantly increasethe state space. In addition, it
45:1678:95098:18:18:71:539:243:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:would to require a bulk of additional assumptions and complicate the theoretical results.
45:1679:95187:18:18:71:539:225:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:We therefore chose to concentrate on the two simple mechanisms from Proposition 1 in the
45:1680:95275:18:35:71:329:207:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:main paper and leave this issue to future research.

47:1746:97149:0:26:71:428:707:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:C:A.3.1  Observations about Cross-Country Busts and Booms
47:1747:97204:26:18:71:539:681:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:From Tables A.5 and A.6 we make the following observations. Countries that, similar to
47:1748:97290:18:18:71:539:663:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:the U.S., have large depressions and large baby busts throughout the 1930s are Canada,
47:1749:97376:18:18:71:540:645:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:Australia and New Zealand.  Countries with slightly smallerdepressions and baby busts
47:1750:97461:18:18:71:540:627:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:are Germany, the U.K. and France.  Note that France has a late depression (late 1930s)
47:1751:97547:18:18:71:539:609:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:and correspondingly, a late baby bust while Germany and the U.K. have large depressions
47:1752:97634:18:18:71:540:591:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:in the early 1930s and correspondingly large baby busts early on.  For the Netherlands,
47:1753:97721:18:17:71:540:573:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:Belgium and Switzerland, the depression is noticeable onlyif the effects of wars are taken
47:1754:97811:17:18:71:540:556:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:into account when detrending (Table A.6). For Southern European countries, Italy, Portugal
47:1755:97901:18:18:71:539:538:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:and Spain, both the depression as well as the baby bust are small or non-existent, depending
47:1756:97993:18:18:71:539:520:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:on detrending method. For Northern European countries, Denmark, Norway and Sweden,
47:1757:98075:18:18:71:539:502:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:large baby busts can be observed, despite the fact that the depression is small or non-existent
47:1758:98170:18:18:71:500:484:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:depending on detrending method. These observations rationalize results in Table 6.
47:1759:98252:18:15:89:539:466:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:Because of the last observation, when analyzing baby booms in the main text, we use
47:1760:98335:15:21:74:539:451:12:g_d0_f12:12:g_d0_f2:B:̂CBR1930srather than̂GDP1930sas the independent variable. This allows us to determine
47:1761:98420:21:18:71:539:430:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:whether baby busts are correlated with subsequent baby booms, independently of their cause.
47:1762:98511:18:18:89:540:412:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:Indeed, from Tables A.7 and A.8, all three Northern countries display substantial baby
47:1763:98597:18:18:71:540:394:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:booms in the 1950s, though potentially mitigated by a recession in the 1950s. Similarly,
47:1764:98685:18:18:71:540:376:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:Central European and Southern European countries, all havesmall baby booms in the late
47:1765:98771:18:18:71:539:358:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:1950s with GDP significantly below trend for most detrendingmethods. Southern European
47:1766:98857:18:17:71:540:340:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:countries have somewhat of a baby boom. The largest baby booms are to be observed in
47:1767:98941:17:18:71:539:323:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:the U.S., Canada, Australia and New Zealand where the baby bust was particularly large.
47:1768:99028:18:35:71:380:305:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:These observations rationalize the weaker results in Table7.
47:1769:99088:35:26:71:368:270:14:g_d0_f1:14:g_d0_f1:C:A.4  Sensitivity to changes in parameters
47:1770:99129:26:18:71:539:244:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:we compute how elasticities and fertility levels change when we change parameter values
47:1771:99216:18:18:71:540:226:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:in Table 2. Results are reported in Table A.9. For each parameter change, we report two
47:1772:99303:18:18:71:539:208:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:cases.  First, we give results in the case where costs of children remain at their baseline
47:1773:99394:18:17:71:539:190:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:value, which leads to varying fertility levels in steady state. Second, we recalibrate to the
47:1774:99487:17:18:71:539:173:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:target population growth by adjusting costs of children accordingly.  Results show that
47:1775:99574:18:18:71:540:155:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:within a reasonable range of parameter values, the elasticity of contemporaneous fertility to
47:1776:99667:18:18:71:539:137:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:productivity shocks ranges from 1.6 to 1.9 in the goods cost case and 0.95 to 1.2 for the time
47:1777:99761:18:18:71:539:119:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:cost case, while the elasticity of lagged fertility ranges from -1.25 to -1.9 in the goods cost
47:1778:99856:18:18:71:539:101:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:case and -0.7 to -1.2 for the time cost case, whether we recalibrate or not. Thus, the results
47:1779:99950:18:0:71:342:83:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:don’t seem to be very sensitive to parameter choices.
